    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="7395ed1623cb4e3cae77babfe895cab9">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '432d17f4-1ae7-4dd9-a726-8ff54e3c66d4'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Cui/Xinwei">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Xu/Qun" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Qun Xu<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Qun Xu</p>
                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">
                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a class="sm-account__link" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:qunxu@zzu.edu.cn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-ic_mail_solid"></i><span>qunxu@zzu.edu.cn</span></a></li>
                     </ul>

                     <p>Henan Institute of Advanced Technology, Zhengzhou University, Zhengzhou, 450003 P. R. China</p>
                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:jianhuazhu@zzu.edu.cn"><span>jianhuazhu@zzu.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:qunxu@zzu.edu.cn"><span>qunxu@zzu.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Xu/Qun">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d46472674" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sluggish ion diffusion of large sodium ions is one of the main drawbacks challenging the development of metal selenides-based anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). A spin-state regulating strategy is first proposed in this work to lift the p-band center (ɛ<sub>p</sub>) of Se for a fast Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetic in (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>. By utilizing the electron transfer from Cu to Co, the π-symmetry t<sub>2g</sub> of Co is fully occupied to decrease the spin polarization. The resultant electron repulsion between Co and Se weakens Co–Se bond to lift the ɛ<sub>p</sub> of Se. The enhanced sodium adsorption energy effectively accelerates the ion transfer at the active material–electrolyte interface. As a result, the (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC electrode exhibits an superior sodium storage performance with a capacity of 445&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, 312&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and 363&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 10.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The insight into the working mechanism of regulating spin-state of metals to lift the p-band center of Se can provide guidelines for the development of both metal selenides-based anode material and high-performance SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202405642-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202405642-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Guided by global strategies aiming at sustaining development, society is progressively shifting toward renewable energy sources, fostering the swift advancement of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with abundant resources, economical production, and analogous reaction mechanisms to lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0001_R_d46472661e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature Energy Storage Mater. Mater. Chem. Phys. Adv. Sci. Electrochem. Energy Rev.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the larger ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02&nbsp;Å) than Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76&nbsp;Å) results in sluggish chemical reaction kinetics, poor reversibility, and severe volume expansion, thus, delivering poor rate performance and undesirable cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0002_R_d46472661e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Small Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. ACS Nano Adv. Sci.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among tremendous reported SIBs anode materials, metal selenides (MSe<sub>x</sub>) possess high theoretical capacities, low electronegativity, and narrow band gap. The weaker bonding energy of M–Se than that of M–O, M–P, and M–S contributes to a weak polarization and high electrochemical reversibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0003_R_d46472661e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, their development was still trapped by the limited ionic/electronic conductivity and substantial volume fluctuations during the repetitive sodiation/desodiation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0004_R_d46472661e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Eng. J. Rare Met.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Incorporating carbon materials and designing three-dimensional structures were the two common strategies to alleviate these drawbacks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0005_R_d46472661e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> But the surface ion adsorption and the transport of ions and electrons within the bulk phase of metal selenides remains a challenge for the conversion reactions, requiring novel electronic structure regulation method to achieve an improved reaction depth and rate performance.</p>
         
         <p>It is widely recognized that selenium atoms play a pivotal role in facilitating the adsorption of sodium ions in metal selenides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0006_R_d46472661e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Namely, modulating the electronic structure of selenium atoms is imperative for fundamentally improving the adsorption and transportation of Na<sup>+</sup>. Adjusting the band center of nonmetallic atoms can potentially augment cation adsorption.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0007_R_d46472661e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It was reported that an enhanced p-band center of oxygen atoms in vanadium oxide resulted in an increased adsorption energy to zinc ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0008_R_d46472661e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Powder Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Studies have shown several effective methods to regulate the p-band center of non-metallic atoms, including vacancy engineering,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0009_R_d46472661e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Appl. Catal. B-Environ.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ionic intercalation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0010_R_d46472661e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Res.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and hybrid orbitals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0011_R_d46472661e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nano Lett.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, modulating the bonds between metal atoms and non-metal atoms was also proposed to achieve a tunable band center in the nonmetal atoms. For instance, the metal atom (Cu, Mg, Ni, Co, and Zn) imposes an effect on the Mn–O bond in MnO<sub>2</sub> to obtain administrable p-band center of O atom. The incorporation of Cu led to an elevated d-band center of −2.24&nbsp;eV for an increased hydrogen adsorption energy of −3.74&nbsp;eV.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0007_R_d46472661e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Inspired by the recent advancements in spin polarization engineering,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0012_R_d46472661e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> a new route was proposed to adjust the p band center of Se atoms in metal selenides, and meanwhile, expand the application scenarios of electron-spin regulation. CoSe<sub>2</sub> was selected as the object in this work owing to the high theoretical capacity, high conductivity and low ion transfer rate as well as the high magnetic moment resulted from the abundant unpaired electrons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0013_R_d46472661e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to lift the p-band center of Se atoms, the Co–Se bonds should be weakened to lower the orbital hybridization. It can be achieved by decreasing the spin polarization (i.e., magnetic moment) of Co atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0014_R_d46472661e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As reported, copper atoms with low magnetic moment can be an electron donator to provide electrons to the host materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0008_R_d46472661e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Powder Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From this point, introducing Cu atoms into CoSe<sub>2</sub> can be a potential solution to reduce the number of unpaired electrons for a lower spin polarization, thereby, achieving an elevated p band center of Se to facilitate the sodium adsorption and diffusion.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, copper atoms were introduced into CoSe<sub>2</sub> to regulate the crystal and electronic structures for a fast sodium-storage kinetic. With the assistance of theoretical prediction, a core–shell structured (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>@nitrogen-doped carbon ((Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC) composite was synthesized based on the combination of templating method, ion-exchange, and selenization processes. The nitrogen-doped carbon shell enabled a fast electron transfer to the metal selenides and a good stress relieve during the charge/discharge process. The introduction of copper atoms enabled the phase change from the orthorhombic CoSe<sub>2</sub> to trogtalite (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> with cubic structure, which was conducive to benefit the ion diffusion rate within the bulk phase. More importantly, the electron transfer from Cu to Co, confirmed by the DFT and XPS results, was able to decrease the number of unpaired electrons in Co atoms for a low spin polarization and weak Co–Se band, thereby, lifting the p-band center of Se and the sodium adsorption energy. Consequently, the electrochemical reaction kinetic of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> was well improved to give a superior sodium storage performance.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202405642-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202405642-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were conducted to theoretically investigate the electronic structures of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC, and the influences of introducing Cu atoms on the electrochemical properties. Based on the optimized models in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a> (Supporting Information), total density of states (TDOS, <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0001">1a</a>) and partial density of states (PDOS, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b–d</a>, Supporting Information) of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> around the Fermi level was higher that the CoSe<sub>2</sub> and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se, illustrated an slightly enhanced electron mobility. The differential charge density image of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0001">1b</a>) demonstrated the accumulation of electron around Co atoms and the depletion of electrons at Cu atoms. It offers evidence of spontaneous electron transfer from Cu to Co in the cause of balancing the Fermi levels,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0015_R_d46472661e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Storage Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which should be able to regulate the spin polarization of Co atoms. The spin polarization was then examined by electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR), in which the peak density can be used to determine the number of unpaired electrons. According to the magnetic-induced electron spin energy level splitting formulation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0016_R_d46472661e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> it was clear that the magnetic moment of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> was decreased compared with that of CoSe<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0001">1c</a>). Besides, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se without unpaired electrons only displayed a signal intensity close to zero. The computed magnetic moments (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0001">1d</a>) matched well with the EPR results, illustrating the role of introduced Cu atoms on decreasing the number of unpaired electrons in (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> through the d–p–d hybridization between Cu, Se, and Co atoms. The magnetic moment of MSe<sub>x</sub> was all came from the metal atoms in them since the electrons in Se atoms are all paired. The resultant low spin polarization of metal atoms in (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> weakened their bonding with Se atoms to lift the p-band center (ɛ<sub>p</sub>) of Se atoms (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0001">1e</a>). As a result, (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> achieved the highest sodium adsorption energy of −3.377&nbsp;eV, by adjusting the s–p orbital hybridization between Se and Na (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0001">1f</a>). This strong interaction could also be supported by the highest decrease in the magnetic moment of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> after sodium adsorption (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0001">1d</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202405642-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/934ad382-750f-42fe-b74a-6ea4398a8afc/adfm202405642-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/934ad382-750f-42fe-b74a-6ea4398a8afc/adfm202405642-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2719e871-263b-404f-b958-671351f97c17/adfm202405642-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/934ad382-750f-42fe-b74a-6ea4398a8afc/adfm202405642-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202405642-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202405642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Total density of states (TDOS) plots on the (111) surface of CoSe<sub>2</sub>, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se, and (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>; b) differential charge density diagram of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>; c) EPR spectra, d) magnetic moment analysis before/after adsorption Na, e) p-band centers (ɛ<sub>p</sub>) of the Se 3d orbitals; and f) the sodium adsorption energies of CoSe<sub>2</sub>, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se, and (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC composite was synthesized through a combination of templating methods, ion exchange and selenization processes, as illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0002">2a</a>. Initially, a solid Zn–Co metal–organic framework (MOF) was prepared (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a,b</a>, Supporting Information) by the coordination effect. Then Cu<sup>2+</sup> and phenolic resin (PF) layer were successively introduced by ion-exchange (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2d,e</a>, Supporting Information) and polymerization (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2g,h</a>, Supporting Information). TEM images clearly demonstrated the solid nature of the Zn-Co MOF (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information), the Zn–Co/MOF/Cu (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2f</a>, Supporting Information), and the Zn–Co MOF/Cu@polymer (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2i</a>, Supporting Information). Subsequently, core–shell polyhedron particles designated as Co–Cu/NC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a-c</a>, Supporting Information) were produced during the thermolysis process, with uniform carbon shells of ≈42.4&nbsp;nm and metal cores with a size of ≈46.1&nbsp;nm. HRTEM images in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3e-f</a> (Supporting Information) and X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) have confirmed the existence of both Cu and Co in the cores. The absence of Zn should be attributed to the ion-exchange process and the evaporation under the high annealing temperature of 900&nbsp;°C. Finally, the polyhedron-shaped core–shell structured (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC product was achieved through a selenization process under 500&nbsp;°C. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0002">2b–e</a> depict the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) images of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC, showcasing the well-maintained polyhedron shape and yolk–shell structure with dimensions ranging from 200 to 400&nbsp;nm. Specifically, the uniform particles with a size of ≈50.8&nbsp;nm are distinctly visible within the cavity. In contrast, the control samples without cobalt nitrate hexahydrate or copper acetate exhibited a noticeable irregular polyhedron appearance (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0002">2f</a> showing a distinct crystal lattice with a fringe spacing of 0.264&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the lattice planes (210) of trogtalite (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>. Correspondingly, XRD pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0002">2g</a>) of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> demonstrated its cubic crystal phase (JCPDS No. 25-0253), in comparison to the control samples with Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC (Cubic, JCPDS No. 88-2043) and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC (Orthorhombic, JCPDS No. 53-0449). The construction of trogtalite (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> may be induced by the introduction of Cu atoms, which was able to facilitate the rapid storage of sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0013_R_d46472661e731" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The corresponding elemental mapping images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0002">2h</a> revealed the distribution of Cu, Co, and Se elements in (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC, confirming the successful selenization of the metal cores.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202405642-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/52192fbe-f251-4fc1-8cef-6194208efd75/adfm202405642-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/52192fbe-f251-4fc1-8cef-6194208efd75/adfm202405642-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ff96bc07-e6b3-4647-8ac7-7669c102156c/adfm202405642-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/52192fbe-f251-4fc1-8cef-6194208efd75/adfm202405642-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202405642-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202405642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Illustration of synthetic route for polyhedron-shaped yolk–shell structured (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC nanoparticles. b,c) SEM images at different magnifications, d,e) STEM images, f) HRTEM image, and h) corresponding EDS mappings of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC. (g) XRD patterns of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC, and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Raman spectra in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0003">3a</a> confirmed the existence of metal selenides and carbon in the three composites. The two main peaks at 1350 and 1582&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> can be identified as the D band for defect-induced disordered carbon and G band for sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized graphitic carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0017_R_d46472661e784" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The intensity ratio between D and G band (I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub>) was calculated to be 2.98 for (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC, 2.67 for Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC, and 2.79 for CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC, illustrating the highest amount of defective carbon and active sites in (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0018_R_d46472661e803" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the phonon vibration frequency of A<sub>1g</sub> (267&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) in (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC was blue-shifted compared with the control samples. According to previous studies on phonon dynamics of topological insulators, it indicated the existence of magnetic order and lattice distortion in (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0019_R_d46472661e817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Crystals">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was carried out to explore the elemental valence and chemical bonding states (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The high-resolution Co 2p spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0003">3b</a>) showed the two spin-orbit doublet peaks of 779.7 and 794.9&nbsp;eV that could be described by Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0020_R_d46472661e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Appl. Catal. B: Environ.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, the two major peaks of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC nanostructure at 952.7 and 932.8&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0003">3c</a>) could be attributed to Cu 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Cu 2p<sub>3/2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0021_R_d46472661e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC, the higher binding energies of Cu 2p peaks for (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC revealed the decreased electron occupation with declined electron-donation ability. On the other hand, the spectrum of Co 2p peaks exhibits a shift to lower binding energies in comparison to CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC, illustrating an increase in the electron density of the Co atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0022_R_d46472661e863" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nanoscale">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XPS data were well matched with the DFT results, substantiating the presence of electron transfer occurring in (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> to modulate the electronic structure of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0023_R_d46472661e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the Se 3d spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0003">3d</a>, the Se 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Se 3d<sub>5/2</sub> peaks of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC were slightly shifted to a lower binding energies, indicating a weak M–Se bonds in comparison to that of CoSe<sub>2</sub> and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0024_R_d46472661e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202405642-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f4491627-3d1a-48c1-8547-2c7c32b33195/adfm202405642-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f4491627-3d1a-48c1-8547-2c7c32b33195/adfm202405642-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/278d96e3-819a-449e-a594-5bf485a85f6e/adfm202405642-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f4491627-3d1a-48c1-8547-2c7c32b33195/adfm202405642-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202405642-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202405642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Raman patterns of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC; b) Co 2p XPS spectra for CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC and (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC; c) Cu 2p XPS spectra for Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC; d) Se 3d XPS spectra for CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC; e) N<sub>2</sub> absorption/desorption isotherms curves; and f) pore size distributions of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The high-resolution C 1s and N 1s spectra of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC were given in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7b,c</a> (Supporting Information), which were all derived from the MOF and PF polymer layer. The presence of C–N bonds was conductive for achieving a rapid charge transfer, and may also provide additional active sites for sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0025_R_d46472661e955" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The specific surface area and pore distribution of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC were checked by N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0003">3e,f</a>). They all exhibits a type-IV adsorption–desorption isotherm with a distinctive hysteresis loop, demonstrating the existence of micro-/mesoporous structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0026_R_d46472661e972" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC possessed a largest specific surface area of 42.09&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> compared to 23.42&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and 24.62&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC. Furthermore, the pore size distribution determined by Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) models demonstrated that (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC consists more micropores, mesopores, and macropores than Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC. Such a highly porous structure is beneficial to the exposure of active sites and the penetration of electrolyte ions, thus promoting the reaction kinetics for an enhanced sodium storage performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0017_R_d46472661e1004" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Electrochemical performances of the three composites were examined in half cells with metal sodium as counter electrodes. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0004">4a</a> shows the initial four cyclic voltammograms (CV) curves of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC electrode collected over a voltage window of 0.1–2.5&nbsp;V at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. During the first discharge process, the reduction peaks at 1.54, 1.07, and 0.32&nbsp;V were attributed to the intercalation reaction forming Na<sub>x</sub>(Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>, the conversion reaction to generate Na<sub>2</sub>Se, and the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer owing to the decomposition of electrolyte, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0027_R_d46472661e1028" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Mater. Chem. A">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the charge process, the oxidation peaks located at 1.58 and 1.81&nbsp;V correspond to the reversible reaction transforming Na<sub>2</sub>Se to Na<sub>x</sub>(Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> and finally (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>, respectively. The nearly overlapping CV curves of the subsequent scans indicating the excellent reversibility of electrochemical conversion reaction because of the synergistic effects between the yolk–shell structure and the single-phase bimetallic selenides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0028_R_d46472661e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The initial four cyclic CV curves of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC electrodes at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a,b</a>, Supporting Information) exhibited the similar characteristics compared to the (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC anode, featuring two major redox peaks. Furthermore, the normalized CV curve of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC at the 2nd cycle (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9c</a>, Supporting Information) shows much larger loops and increased peak area, suggesting that more Na ions were allowed to de-embed or embed.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202405642-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5425797e-8981-4c99-923a-ad74042c1d8f/adfm202405642-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5425797e-8981-4c99-923a-ad74042c1d8f/adfm202405642-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/be568088-57a4-49f1-9198-d1bbfd1a6800/adfm202405642-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5425797e-8981-4c99-923a-ad74042c1d8f/adfm202405642-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202405642-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202405642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The initial four a) CV curves at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> and b) charge–discharge curves at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC electrode. c) Rate performance and d) cycle performance of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC electrodes. e) Long-term cycling performance of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC electrodes at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. f) Rate performance and g) cycling performance at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC||NVP full cells. Insert in (g) shows the digital photo of the full cell to light up 29 red LEDs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0004">4b</a> shows the initial four galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) profiles of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC at a current density of 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The potential plateaus were well matched with the peaks in CV curves. The initial discharge capacities of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC were 816.0, 793.8, and 710.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an initial coulombic efficiency of 59.4%, 51.8%, and 57.8%, respectively. The irreversible capacity loss was mainly caused by the irreversible formation of SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0029_R_d46472661e1129" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Nano">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the subsequent three cycles, GCD curves of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC were nearly overlapping with a coulombic efficiency close to 100%, revealing a better stability than the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), which is consistent with CV results. For the rate performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0004">4c</a>), (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC electrode exhibits high specific discharge capacities of 460, 445, 434, 419, 393, 379, 358, 339, and 312&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, 20, 30, and 50&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In addition, when the current density returned back to 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity was fully restored to 448&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This rate capability of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC surpasses the control samples in this work and many other reported metal selenides (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). This enhancement undoubtedly arises from the enhanced conductivity and the augmented Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption. As for the cycling test, (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC achieved a specific capacity of 443&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0004">4d</a>). Additionally, a notable capacity of 368&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g⁻<sup>1</sup> (99.2%) was achieved after 10&nbsp;000 cycles during the long-term cycling test at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g⁻¹, while its only 285&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and 235&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for the CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0004">4e</a>). Furthermore, based on the supplementary data in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), the capacity of hollow NC electrode was only 112.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, 46.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and 70.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (capacity retention: 88.0%) after 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, suggesting a stable structure and low contribution to the capacity of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC. This excellent performance indicated the significance of synergistic engineering between the unique yolk–shell architecture, the trogtalite crystal and the regulated electronic structure on enhancing the electron/ion transfer and the structural stability.</p>
         
         <p>To further check the capability of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC anode in full sodium-ion battery, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C (NVP@C) cathode was chosen as the counter electrode to assembly a full battery denoted as (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC//NVP@C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a>, Supporting Information). The NVP@C//Na cathodes showed a high voltage plateau in half batteries (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), delivering a good rate capability with a capacity of 77.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g⁻<sup>1</sup> at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and a stable cycling performance with a capacity of 82.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g⁻<sup>1</sup> after 100 cycles at 1A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. CV profiles were firstly conducted to validate the electrochemical process and reversibility of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC//NVP@C full cell (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a>, Supporting Information), which demonstrated an excellent cycling stability. Based on the anode mass, the full cell could give a high reversible capacity of 131&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (71.1%) after 200 cycles at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0004">4f,g</a>). Such a remarkable rate and cycling performance in full cells demonstrated the viability of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC anode for practical application in SIBs. The inset photograph in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0004">4g</a> further the practical potential of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC//NVP@C full cell by illuminating the “ZZU” patterned light-emitting diodes (LED). Additionally, the Ragone plot (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12c</a>, Supporting Information) that compares the energy and power densities of other sodium-ion full cells clearly demonstrates the viability of our (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC//NVP@C full cell by successfully delivering a high energy density of 174.5&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> at a power density of 106.7&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, With the power density increases up to 2133.6&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, the energy density still remains at 76.3&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, further confirming the potential applications of our battery in real practice.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">In order to explore the electrochemical kinetics and gain insight into the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process, CV measurements were performed at different scan rates from 0.1 to 0.9&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0005">5a</a>, the CV curves display similar peaks at various scan rates, indicating the low polarization and superior stability. At a fixed scan rate, the region enclosed by CV curve represents the total charges generated by diffusion-controlled and capacitive-controlled contributions, and the relationship between scan rate (v) and peak current (i) can be expressed by the following equations:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0030_R_d46472661e1289" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Small">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202405642-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

When the <i>b</i> value is close to 0.5, the capacity storage is limited by diffusion-controlled process. As <i>b</i> approaches 1.0, it indicates the domination of capacitive-controlled pseudocapacitive process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0031_R_d46472661e1351" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information) presents the relationship of log(<i>i</i>) and log(<i>v</i>) of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC. The <i>b</i> values fitted for all peaks were close to 1, implying a dominant capacitive-controlled charge storage process. Furthermore, the contributions of diffusion-dominant and capacitive-dominant processes can be quantitatively determined by the following equations:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0032_R_d46472661e1369" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nat. The gradually increased capacitive contribution as the increase of scan rate signifies the superior pseudocapacitive behavior. In comparison, the capacitive-controlled contribution obtained from the <i>CV</i> curves of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC were lower at all scan rates (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16-S17</a>, Supporting Information), evidencing their slower reaction kinetics.</div>

         <div class="paragraph-element">To further evaluate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC electrodes, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests were carried out at a current pulse of 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0005">5d,e</a>). The average <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> was calculated to be 1.38 × 10<sup>−9</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> during the sodiation/desodiation process, which is higher than that of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC (1.03 × 10<sup>−9&nbsp;</sup>cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>) and CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC (7.34 × 10<sup>−10&nbsp;</sup>cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>). In addition, the (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC electrode displays the lowest average overpotentials, suggesting a good Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0005">5f</a> depicts the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) results of the pristine three electrodes and the corresponding equivalent circuit (insert). The Nyquist plots can be divided into two parts including the semicircle in the higher frequency region and the sloping line in the lower frequency region. They corresponds to charge transfer process and the ion diffusion stage, which can be used to determine the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and Warburg diffusion process (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0034_R_d46472661e1663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nano Energy">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC was only ≈55.17&nbsp;Ω, much lower than the ≈78.66&nbsp;Ω for Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/NC and ≈146.3&nbsp;Ω for CoSe<sub>2</sub>/NC. The fast charge transfer property of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC can be derived from the Cu–Se–Co hybridization. The corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) can be determined by the following equations:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0035_R_d46472661e1686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Energy Lett.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202405642-disp-0006"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{Z}_{{\mathrm{re}}}} = R + \sigma {{\omega }^{ - 1/2}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(7)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202405642-disp-0008"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/931d3f9d-29b6-4597-87d7-6cbf98e09420/adfm202405642-math-0008.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202405642:adfm202405642-math-0008" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202405642-math-0008.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>D</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                                    <mo>+</mo>
                                 </msup>
                              </mrow>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mfrac>
                              <mrow>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>R</mi>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </msup>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>T</mi>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </msup>
                              </mrow>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>A</mi>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </msup>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>n</mi>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </msup>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>F</mi>
                                    <mn>4</mn>
                                 </msup>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>C</mi>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </msup>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>σ</mi>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </msup>
                              </mrow>
                           </mfrac>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{D}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}} = \frac{{{{R}^2}{{T}^2}}}{{2{{A}^2}{{n}^2}{{F}^4}{{C}^2}{{\sigma }^2}}}\ \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(8)</span></div>where <i>f</i> is the frequency, <i>A</i> is the area of the electrodes, <i>n</i> is the number of electronic transfers per molecule, <i>C</i> is the molar concentration of Na<sup>+</sup>, <i>R</i> is the gas constant (<i>R</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;8.314&nbsp;J&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup>&nbsp;K<sup>−1</sup>), <i>T</i> is the absolute temperature and <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant (<i>F</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;96&nbsp;500&nbsp;C&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup>). According to the image of <i>Z</i>’ versus ω<sup>−1/2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0005">5g</a>), the slopes (σ) fitted for (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC was the lowest among the three samples, indicating a highest <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> that in coincidence with the GITT result.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0036_R_d46472661e1816" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Small">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The thoroughly verified high Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient was certainly attributed to the introducing Cu atoms that adjusting the crystal and electronic structures of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC. Especially, both <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and σ values decreased after 100 cycles owing to the electrochemical activation process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0037_R_d46472661e1832" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC electrode still exhibited the lowest <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and σ, demonstrated that its excellent electrochemical kinetic could be well maintained during the cycling process.</div>
         
         <p>In situ XRD measurements were conducted to identify the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism and phase evolution of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC electrode during the initial charging-discharging processes at a current density of 150&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0006">6a</a>). The peaks situated at 30.3°, 33.9°, and 37.1° at the pristine state were came from of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>. During the intercalation stage, the signals of the (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> gradually vanished. New peaks emerged at 22.6°, 37.3°, and 44.1° were belong to the (111), (220), and (311) planes of Na<sub>2</sub>Se (JCPDS No. 77-2155), respectively. They indicated the fully occurrence of conversion reaction between (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup>. Nevertheless, no obvious diffraction peaks of Co<sup>0</sup> and Cu<sup>0</sup> were observed in the XRD pattern due to the low content and small particle size.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0038_R_d46472661e1873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nat. Commun.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the subsequent desodiation process, the peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>Se gradually faded, suggesting the happening of reversed conversion reaction. In the fully charged state, the peaks of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> can hardly be observed, which might be attributed to the low crystallinity in the rebuilt (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0039_R_d46472661e1885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To explore the stability and dynamic evolution of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC, ex situ SEM and TEM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information) were employed to collect the structures at different voltages and scales. During the reversible conversion reactions, five representative points were selected (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19a</a>, Supporting Information). Cross-sectional SEM images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19b</a>, left, Supporting Information) illustrated the negligible volume change of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC anodes with a low expansion rate of only ∼7%. The ex situ TEM images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19b</a>, middle, Supporting Information) show that the core–shell structure remains unchanged, providing adequate void space for accommodating the volume changes of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>. In addition, the HR-TEM images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19b</a>, right, Supporting Information) illustrated the generation of Na<sub>2</sub>Se and metal particles as well as the re-construction of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>. It highlighted the structural evolution of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC during charge/discharge cycles and its good reversibility, aligned well with the in situ XRD result. The (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC was further characterized by SEM and TEM after 100 cycles at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0006">6b–d</a>). It is evident that the polyhedron-shaped yolk–shell structure was well maintained, with no obvious structural crack or collapse. HRTEM in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0006">6e</a> displayed the representative (210) planes of trogtalite (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>, further supported the reconstruction of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>. Element distributions in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0006">6f</a> were similar as that in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0002">2g</a>, illustrating the well stability of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC composite after the cyclic conversion reactions. XPS analysis was further conducted to bring complementary information on the chemical states of the (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC after 100 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). The binding energies of copper, cobalt, and selenium maintained the same as their initial states. It indicated that the Co-Se-Cu structure was stable enough to continuously induce the electron transfer from Cu to Co for modulating the spin polarization of Co atoms, thereby raising the center of the p-band in selenium throughout the cycling tests.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202405642-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0bfef003-98ab-4230-8555-0101f3cf03c2/adfm202405642-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0bfef003-98ab-4230-8555-0101f3cf03c2/adfm202405642-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/533194ba-beb5-4845-b541-df9b8082b727/adfm202405642-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0bfef003-98ab-4230-8555-0101f3cf03c2/adfm202405642-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202405642-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202405642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ XRD patterns of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC during the charging/discharging processes. b) SEM images, c,d) TEM images, e) HRTEM image, and f) EDS elemental mappings of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>/NC after 100 cycles at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. g) Schematic illustrating the mechanism of modulating the electronic structure of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> for enhanced sodium adsorption energy.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Take all the above results into consideration, the excellent sodium storage performance of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> can be mainly attributed to the excellent ion diffusion kinetic that enabled by the increased sodium adsorption energy. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0006">6g</a> illustrated the mechanism for modulating the electronic structure of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>. Co<sup>2+</sup> in CoSe<sub>2</sub> and Cu<sup>+</sup> in Cu<sub>2</sub>Se own a valence electron configuration of t<sub>2g</sub><sup>5</sup>e<sub>g</sub><sup>2</sup> with high spin and t<sub>2g</sub><sup>6</sup>e<sub>g</sub><sup>4</sup> with no spin, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0012_R_d46472661e2007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0014_R_d46472661e2007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0040_R_d46472661e2007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Two Co<sup>2+</sup> with partially occupied d-orbitals interacts with the bridging Se<sup>2−</sup> via balanced π-donation in CoSe<sub>2</sub>. As for (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>, the electron transfer from Cu to Co, confirmed by the DFT calculation and XPS analysis, resulted in less unpaired electrons and decreased spin polarization. The fully occupied π-symmetry t<sub>2g</sub> contributed to electron repulsion between Co atom and Se atom to weaken the Co–Se bond. The weakened metal–Se bonds contributed to a decreased orbital hybridization, thereby, lifting the ɛ<sub>p</sub> of Se from −3.83&nbsp;eV for CoSe<sub>2</sub> to −3.50&nbsp;eV for (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0001">1e</a>). As illustrated in the third image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405642-fig-0006">6g</a>, the increased ɛ<sub>p</sub> of Se in (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> decreased the occupancy degree of Na–Se antibonding sigma orbital (σ*).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405642-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405642-bib-0007_R_d46472661e2041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Eventually, the interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and Se in metal selenides was enhanced to display a high sodium adsorption energy. The accelerated the ion transfer at the electrode–electrolyte interface helped to construct a complete and rapid ion transfer pathway from the electrolyte to the bulk phase of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>, by combining the advantages of the yolk–shell structure and trogtalite crystal. Besides, the chemical environment and electronic states remain unchanged after cycling, indicating that the spin-polarization modulating strategy was effective throughout the cycling process. The achieved excellent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetic brought in the excellent sodium storage performance of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202405642-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202405642-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles embedded hollow nitrogen-doped carbon NCs were elaborately synthesized by a templating, ion-exchange and selenization process. XPS analysis revealed the electron transfer from Cu to Co that could occupy the π-symmetry t<sub>2g</sub> to weaken the M–Se bonds, which was in good agreement with the DFT calculation results. The p-band center of Se was then lifted from −3.83 to −3.5&nbsp;eV, with an augmented sodium adsorption energy from −2.433 to −3.377&nbsp;eV. Such an accelerated ion transfer at the active material–electrolyte interface completed a crucial link in ion diffusion. A complete and rapid pathway was constructed from electrolyte to the bulk phase of (Co,Cu)Se<sub>2</sub>, by combing the structural advantages from yolk–shell architecture and trogtalite phase. The multiscale structures were stable enough upon the cycling conversion reactions, displaying an excellent electrochemical performance in regard to a high capacity of 445&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, a rate capability of 312&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and a superior cycling performance of 363&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. All in all, this synergistic engineering of architecture and the lifted p-band center of Se by a spin polarization regulating strategy can provide guidance for developing other metal selenides-based anodes for high-performance SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
